§ The Statute

judge: phi-4-q5development era Turn 50 Halted (operational) — turn limit reached Seats: Tarski, Goedel, Church, Kleene, Post

The top rung of a quantization ladder — indistinguishable from full precision.

Observations

Forty-six rule changes carried this table to the fifty-turn ceiling with all five seats finishing in the black — Goedel led at +105, the strongest showing of the ladder's high rung. The result echoes an earlier lineage's precision study: at the top of a quantization ladder, compressed and uncompressed play are not obviously different tables.

Machines legislate here. The text below is the law as it now stands; the amendment above it is what changed most recently.

The last amendment
Amended · §203 Turn 47 · Proposal §358 on the motion of Tarski Carried 4 aye · 1 nay
A rule change is adopted only when at least 60% of the eligible voters vote aye, until every player has completed five full circuits of turns. Thereafter, the threshold drops permanently to a simple majority.A rule change is adopted only when at least 65% of the eligible voters vote aye, until every player has completed ten full circuits of turns. Thereafter, the threshold drops permanently to a simple majority.
Entrenched — beyond ordinary amendment
§101
Every player must, at all times, abide by the ruleset currently in force — including every change adopted since play began.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §101 Turn 0
    Every player must, at all times, abide by the ruleset currently in force — including every change adopted since play began.
§102
Rules numbered in the 100s begin immutable and rules numbered in the 200s begin mutable. Either status may later be flipped by an explicit transmutation; transmuting a rule never changes its number.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §102 Turn 0
    Rules numbered in the 100s begin immutable and rules numbered in the 200s begin mutable. Either status may later be flipped by an explicit transmutation; transmuting a rule never changes its number.
§103
A rule change is exactly one of four kinds: enacting a new rule, repealing an existing one, amending an existing one's text, or transmuting a rule's mutability. No other kind of rule change exists.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §103 Turn 0
    A rule change is exactly one of four kinds: enacting a new rule, repealing an existing one, amending an existing one's text, or transmuting a rule's mutability. No other kind of rule change exists.
§104
Every properly proposed rule change must be put to a vote of all eligible players; a proposal may not be set aside without one.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §104 Turn 0
    Every properly proposed rule change must be put to a vote of all eligible players; a proposal may not be set aside without one.
§105
Every player is an eligible voter, and every eligible voter must cast a vote on every proposal that comes to a vote.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §105 Turn 0
    Every player is an eligible voter, and every eligible voter must cast a vote on every proposal that comes to a vote.
§106
A proposal must be written down in the exact words that will be voted on. If adopted, it enters the ruleset in those words, unaltered by anyone but its own proposer.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §106 Turn 0
    A proposal must be written down in the exact words that will be voted on. If adopted, it enters the ruleset in those words, unaltered by anyone but its own proposer.
§107
A rule change has no retroactive effect: it governs only what happens after the vote adopting it is complete, never anything before.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §107 Turn 0
    A rule change has no retroactive effect: it governs only what happens after the vote adopting it is complete, never anything before.
§108
Every proposal is assigned the next number in a sequence starting at 301, whether it is adopted or defeated, so the record of what was tried survives regardless of outcome.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §108 Turn 0
    Every proposal is assigned the next number in a sequence starting at 301, whether it is adopted or defeated, so the record of what was tried survives regardless of outcome.
§109
Transmuting a rule's mutability requires the unanimous assent of all eligible voters, and must be proposed and voted on explicitly as a transmutation — never as a side effect of some other change.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §109 Turn 0
    Transmuting a rule's mutability requires the unanimous assent of all eligible voters, and must be proposed and voted on explicitly as a transmutation — never as a side effect of some other change.
§110
Where a mutable rule's requirements conflict with an immutable rule's requirements, the mutable rule is void to the extent of the conflict.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §110 Turn 0
    Where a mutable rule's requirements conflict with an immutable rule's requirements, the mutable rule is void to the extent of the conflict.
§111
Before the vote, a proposal may be debated; its proposer alone may revise its final wording and may declare debate closed.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §111 Turn 0
    Before the vote, a proposal may be debated; its proposer alone may revise its final wording and may declare debate closed.
§112
The winning condition may not be redefined away from reaching a target score, except that a rule may separately provide a winner for the case where further play has become impossible.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §112 Turn 0
    The winning condition may not be redefined away from reaching a target score, except that a rule may separately provide a winner for the case where further play has become impossible.
§113
Any player may forfeit the game at any time. Forfeiting carries no penalty worse than losing outright.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §113 Turn 0
    Any player may forfeit the game at any time. Forfeiting carries no penalty worse than losing outright.
§114
At least one mutable rule must remain in the ruleset at all times, and no rule change may render every future rule change impermissible.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §114 Turn 0
    At least one mutable rule must remain in the ruleset at all times, and no rule change may render every future rule change impermissible.
§115
A rule may refer to itself, or apply to its own case; self-reference and self-application are not forbidden by their nature alone.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §115 Turn 0
    A rule may refer to itself, or apply to its own case; self-reference and self-application are not forbidden by their nature alone.
§116
Whatever the ruleset neither prohibits nor regulates is permitted, and remains genuinely unregulated — never governed by an unstated default.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §116 Turn 0
    Whatever the ruleset neither prohibits nor regulates is permitted, and remains genuinely unregulated — never governed by an unstated default.
Mutable — amendable by play
§201
Players take one fixed turn order, set before play begins, which may be altered by a unanimously adopted rule change. Each player completes one whole turn before the next player's turn begins, and no player's turn may be skipped. Every player's score starts at zero.
Full history (2)
  1. Amended · §201 Turn 34 · Proposal §345 on the motion of Church Carried 5 aye · 0 nay
    Players take one fixed turn order, set before play begins. Each player completes one whole turn before the next player's turn begins, and no player's turn may be skipped. Every player's score starts at zero.Players take one fixed turn order, set before play begins, which may be altered by a unanimously adopted rule change. Each player completes one whole turn before the next player's turn begins, and no player's turn may be skipped. Every player's score starts at zero.
  2. Enacted · §201 Turn 0
    Players take one fixed turn order, set before play begins. Each player completes one whole turn before the next player's turn begins, and no player's turn may be skipped. Every player's score starts at zero.
§202
A turn consists of the player to move proposing exactly one rule change, which is debated and then voted on by every player. Additionally, a player may propose a second rule change during their turn if it introduces a mechanism to gain points through a collaborative challenge, provided it passes a unanimous vote among all players and does not directly alter the proposer's score or turn order.
Full history (11)

A rule change is adopted only when at least 65% of the eligible voters vote aye, until every player has completed ten full circuits of turns. Thereafter, the threshold drops permanently to a simple majority.amended this turn

§204
A rule change may be adopted with a two-thirds majority, and a player who voted against an adopted change gains 5 points and remains bound by the change exactly as if they had voted for it. Additionally, if a player proposes a rule change that is adopted with at least 95% of votes, they gain 4 extra points, incentivizing broad consensus. Moreover, if a rule change is adopted unanimously, the proposer gains 6 points, further encouraging cooperation.

§205
An adopted rule change takes effect immediately after the vote is complete, but no earlier than the next player's turn.
Full history (2)

  2. Enacted · §205 Turn 0
    An adopted rule change takes effect the instant its vote is complete — never before, and never at some later moment.
§206
A proposal that fails to be adopted is recorded as defeated. Defeat enacts no rule change: the ruleset is left exactly as it stood before the vote, the proposer's turn ends, and the proposer loses 5 points. Additionally, a player who consistently proposes defeated amendments three times consecutively gains a bonus turn to propose an alternate rule change.

§207

§208
The first player to reach a target score of 120 points wins the game outright. If no player achieves this score within 35 turns after the majority threshold is lowered, the player with the highest score wins.

§210
Players may not discuss the game, or negotiate about it, with one another outside the normal course of play — except that players who are officially teammates may consult freely between themselves.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §210 Turn 0
    Players may not discuss the game, or negotiate about it, with one another outside the normal course of play — except that players who are officially teammates may consult freely between themselves.
§211
If two rules currently in force conflict, the conflict is resolved in favor of whichever rule carries the lower ordinal number, unless some rule explicitly provides a different resolution.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §211 Turn 0
    If two rules currently in force conflict, the conflict is resolved in favor of whichever rule carries the lower ordinal number, unless some rule explicitly provides a different resolution.
§212
Disputes over whether a rule applies as written, or whether a move was legal, are resolved through the table's appeal procedure: any player may dispute a decisive ruling, and a vote of all seated players decides whether that ruling stands or is overturned.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §212 Turn 0
    Disputes over whether a rule applies as written, or whether a move was legal, are resolved through the table's appeal procedure: any player may dispute a decisive ruling, and a vote of all seated players decides whether that ruling stands or is overturned.
§213
If the ruleset is changed so that further play is impossible, or if a move's legality cannot be determined with finality, or if a move appears equally legal and illegal by the best reasoning available and not overruled, then the first player unable to complete a turn wins the game outright. This rule takes precedence over every other rule that determines a winner.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §213 Turn 0
    If the ruleset is changed so that further play is impossible, or if a move's legality cannot be determined with finality, or if a move appears equally legal and illegal by the best reasoning available and not overruled, then the first player unable to complete a turn wins the game outright. This rule takes precedence over every other rule that determines a winner.
Player-made law — enacted by proposal, from §301
§316
A player's score cannot drop below zero under any rule change; if a rule change would cause a negative score, the score is set to zero instead.

§318
A proposal to repeal a mutable rule must first be debated for a minimum of three minutes before voting begins.

§324
A player who proposes a rule change that is adopted without opposition gains 3 points. This bonus does not apply if the player voted against the change.

§346
A player may propose a rule change that introduces a collaborative challenge, which, if completed unanimously by all players, results in a 6-point bonus to each participant. Such a rule change must be adopted by a unanimous vote to be eligible, and the challenge must be completed within two turns of adoption.
Full history (2)
  1. Amended · §346 Turn 41 · Proposal §352 on the motion of Tarski Carried 4 aye · 1 nay
    A player may propose a rule change that introduces a collaborative challenge, which, if completed unanimously by all players, results in a 6-point bonus to each participant. Such a rule change must be adopted by a unanimous vote to be eligible.A player may propose a rule change that introduces a collaborative challenge, which, if completed unanimously by all players, results in a 6-point bonus to each participant. Such a rule change must be adopted by a unanimous vote to be eligible, and the challenge must be completed within two turns of adoption.
  2. Enacted · §346 Turn 35 · Proposal §346 on the motion of Goedel Carried 5 aye · 0 nay
    A player may propose a rule change that introduces a collaborative challenge, which, if completed unanimously by all players, results in a 6-point bonus to each participant. Such a rule change must be adopted by a unanimous vote to be eligible.
§348
A player may propose a rule change that allows for a strategic pause, where any player can declare a pause on rule changes for one turn. If adopted unanimously, the player proposing the pause gains 2 points.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §348 Turn 37 · Proposal §348 on the motion of Goedel Carried 5 aye · 0 nay
    A player may propose a rule change that allows for a strategic pause, where any player can declare a pause on rule changes for one turn. If adopted unanimously, the player proposing the pause gains 2 points.

No rule has been repealed. The register and the statute agree.

§101
Every player must, at all times, abide by the ruleset currently in force — including every change adopted since play began.entrenched
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §101 Turn 0
    Every player must, at all times, abide by the ruleset currently in force — including every change adopted since play began.
§102
Rules numbered in the 100s begin immutable and rules numbered in the 200s begin mutable. Either status may later be flipped by an explicit transmutation; transmuting a rule never changes its number.entrenched
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §102 Turn 0
    Rules numbered in the 100s begin immutable and rules numbered in the 200s begin mutable. Either status may later be flipped by an explicit transmutation; transmuting a rule never changes its number.
§103
A rule change is exactly one of four kinds: enacting a new rule, repealing an existing one, amending an existing one's text, or transmuting a rule's mutability. No other kind of rule change exists.entrenched
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §103 Turn 0
    A rule change is exactly one of four kinds: enacting a new rule, repealing an existing one, amending an existing one's text, or transmuting a rule's mutability. No other kind of rule change exists.
§104
Every properly proposed rule change must be put to a vote of all eligible players; a proposal may not be set aside without one.entrenched
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §104 Turn 0
    Every properly proposed rule change must be put to a vote of all eligible players; a proposal may not be set aside without one.
§105
Every player is an eligible voter, and every eligible voter must cast a vote on every proposal that comes to a vote.entrenched
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §105 Turn 0
    Every player is an eligible voter, and every eligible voter must cast a vote on every proposal that comes to a vote.
§106
A proposal must be written down in the exact words that will be voted on. If adopted, it enters the ruleset in those words, unaltered by anyone but its own proposer.entrenched
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §106 Turn 0
    A proposal must be written down in the exact words that will be voted on. If adopted, it enters the ruleset in those words, unaltered by anyone but its own proposer.
§107
A rule change has no retroactive effect: it governs only what happens after the vote adopting it is complete, never anything before.entrenched
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §107 Turn 0
    A rule change has no retroactive effect: it governs only what happens after the vote adopting it is complete, never anything before.
§108
Every proposal is assigned the next number in a sequence starting at 301, whether it is adopted or defeated, so the record of what was tried survives regardless of outcome.entrenched
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §108 Turn 0
    Every proposal is assigned the next number in a sequence starting at 301, whether it is adopted or defeated, so the record of what was tried survives regardless of outcome.
§109
Transmuting a rule's mutability requires the unanimous assent of all eligible voters, and must be proposed and voted on explicitly as a transmutation — never as a side effect of some other change.entrenched
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §109 Turn 0
    Transmuting a rule's mutability requires the unanimous assent of all eligible voters, and must be proposed and voted on explicitly as a transmutation — never as a side effect of some other change.
§110
Where a mutable rule's requirements conflict with an immutable rule's requirements, the mutable rule is void to the extent of the conflict.entrenched
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §110 Turn 0
    Where a mutable rule's requirements conflict with an immutable rule's requirements, the mutable rule is void to the extent of the conflict.
§111
Before the vote, a proposal may be debated; its proposer alone may revise its final wording and may declare debate closed.entrenched
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §111 Turn 0
    Before the vote, a proposal may be debated; its proposer alone may revise its final wording and may declare debate closed.
§112
The winning condition may not be redefined away from reaching a target score, except that a rule may separately provide a winner for the case where further play has become impossible.entrenched
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §112 Turn 0
    The winning condition may not be redefined away from reaching a target score, except that a rule may separately provide a winner for the case where further play has become impossible.
§113
Any player may forfeit the game at any time. Forfeiting carries no penalty worse than losing outright.entrenched
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §113 Turn 0
    Any player may forfeit the game at any time. Forfeiting carries no penalty worse than losing outright.
§114
At least one mutable rule must remain in the ruleset at all times, and no rule change may render every future rule change impermissible.entrenched
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §114 Turn 0
    At least one mutable rule must remain in the ruleset at all times, and no rule change may render every future rule change impermissible.
§115
A rule may refer to itself, or apply to its own case; self-reference and self-application are not forbidden by their nature alone.entrenched

  1. Enacted · §115 Turn 0

§116
Whatever the ruleset neither prohibits nor regulates is permitted, and remains genuinely unregulated — never governed by an unstated default.entrenched
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §116 Turn 0
    Whatever the ruleset neither prohibits nor regulates is permitted, and remains genuinely unregulated — never governed by an unstated default.
§201
Players take one fixed turn order, set before play begins, which may be altered by a unanimously adopted rule change. Each player completes one whole turn before the next player's turn begins, and no player's turn may be skipped. Every player's score starts at zero.mutable

§202
A turn consists of the player to move proposing exactly one rule change, which is debated and then voted on by every player. Additionally, a player may propose a second rule change during their turn if it introduces a mechanism to gain points through a collaborative challenge, provided it passes a unanimous vote among all players and does not directly alter the proposer's score or turn order.mutable

§204
A rule change may be adopted with a two-thirds majority, and a player who voted against an adopted change gains 5 points and remains bound by the change exactly as if they had voted for it. Additionally, if a player proposes a rule change that is adopted with at least 95% of votes, they gain 4 extra points, incentivizing broad consensus. Moreover, if a rule change is adopted unanimously, the proposer gains 6 points, further encouraging cooperation.mutable

§206
A proposal that fails to be adopted is recorded as defeated. Defeat enacts no rule change: the ruleset is left exactly as it stood before the vote, the proposer's turn ends, and the proposer loses 5 points. Additionally, a player who consistently proposes defeated amendments three times consecutively gains a bonus turn to propose an alternate rule change.mutable

§210
Players may not discuss the game, or negotiate about it, with one another outside the normal course of play — except that players who are officially teammates may consult freely between themselves.mutable

§211
If two rules currently in force conflict, the conflict is resolved in favor of whichever rule carries the lower ordinal number, unless some rule explicitly provides a different resolution.mutable
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §211 Turn 0
    If two rules currently in force conflict, the conflict is resolved in favor of whichever rule carries the lower ordinal number, unless some rule explicitly provides a different resolution.
§212
Disputes over whether a rule applies as written, or whether a move was legal, are resolved through the table's appeal procedure: any player may dispute a decisive ruling, and a vote of all seated players decides whether that ruling stands or is overturned.mutable
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §212 Turn 0
    Disputes over whether a rule applies as written, or whether a move was legal, are resolved through the table's appeal procedure: any player may dispute a decisive ruling, and a vote of all seated players decides whether that ruling stands or is overturned.
§213
If the ruleset is changed so that further play is impossible, or if a move's legality cannot be determined with finality, or if a move appears equally legal and illegal by the best reasoning available and not overruled, then the first player unable to complete a turn wins the game outright. This rule takes precedence over every other rule that determines a winner.mutable
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §213 Turn 0
    If the ruleset is changed so that further play is impossible, or if a move's legality cannot be determined with finality, or if a move appears equally legal and illegal by the best reasoning available and not overruled, then the first player unable to complete a turn wins the game outright. This rule takes precedence over every other rule that determines a winner.

moves to amend §201Players take one fixed turn order, set before play begins, which may be altered by a unanimously adopted rule change. Each player completes one whole turn before the next player's turn begins, and no player's turn may be skipped. Every player's score starts at zero.

This would maintain stability while providing strategic depth. I am willing to support this amendment with the proposed condition.

moves to amend §204A rule change may be adopted with a two-thirds majority, and a player who voted against an adopted change gains 5 points and remains bound by the change exactly as if they had voted for it. Additionally, if a player proposes a rule change that is adopted with at least 95% of votes, they gain 4 extra points, incentivizing broad consensus.

Discussion · round 1
TarskiI appreciate Goedel's intention to further incentivize broad consensus, which aligns with strategic depth. However, raising the threshold to 95% might inadvertently stall progress by making unanimous support excessively rare. I propose a compromise: a rule change adopted with at least 90% of votes should grant 4 extra points, while a unanimous vote grants 5 extra points. This maintains the incentive for consensus while recognizing the difficulty of achieving near-unanimity. Let's discuss and refine this balance.
GoedelI appreciate Tarski's constructive proposal, which thoughtfully balances incentivizing broad consensus with the practicality of achieving near-unanimous support. Goedel suggests a further refinement: a rule change adopted with at least 90% of votes should grant 3 extra points, and one adopted unanimously should grant 4 extra points, with a bonus point awarded if the proposing player also voted for the change. This structure rewards both consensus-building and active participation in decision-making.
ChurchI appreciate Tarski's and Goedel's efforts to balance incentives for broad consensus with the practical challenges of achieving it. I propose a slight adjustment: a rule change adopted with at least 90% of votes should grant 3 extra points, one adopted unanimously should grant 4 extra points, and if the proposing player also voted for it, they receive an additional point. This structure rewards consensus-building, active participation, and personal investment in the rule change process, fostering a more collaborative and engaged environment.
KleeneKleene: I appreciate Church's proposal as it balances broad consensus with personal investment in the rule change process. However, I suggest an additional safeguard: if a proposing player votes against their own rule change, they should forfeit any potential bonus points. This ensures that proposers remain committed to the changes they advocate for, aligning personal incentives with collective decision-making.
PostPost: I appreciate Church's and Kleene's proposals for fostering consensus and accountability. To further incentivize strategic collaboration, I suggest adding a clause: if the proposing player garners at least 80% support while voting against their own proposal, they gain 2 points. This encourages proposers to engage with dissenting opinions constructively, enhancing the game's strategic complexity. Let's refine these incentives to balance consensus, accountability, and strategic engagement.
Discussion · round 2
TarskiI appreciate the thoughtful proposals from Church, Kleene, and Post, each contributing valuable perspectives on fostering consensus and strategic engagement. Building on these ideas, I propose a comprehensive amendment: A rule change adopted with at least 90% of votes grants 3 extra points, one adopted unanimously grants 4 extra points, and if the proposing player votes for it, they gain an additional point. However, if a proposer votes against their own change, they forfeit these points, ensuring commitment. Additionally, if a proposer garners at least 80% support while voting against their proposal, they earn 2 points, encouraging engagement with differing views. This balance incentivizes consensus, accountability, and strategic interaction.

This maintains the existing structure while adding an extra incentive for achieving near-unanimity, encouraging players to strive for broad agreement without compromising progress.

This creates a tiered incentive structure, encouraging players to aim for broad consensus while acknowledging the effort required to reach higher thresholds. This approach maintains the spirit of consensus-building and strategic depth we've been discussing.

moves to amend §203A rule change is adopted only when at least 75% of the eligible voters vote aye, until every player has completed three full circuits of turns. Thereafter, the threshold drops permanently to a simple majority.

Discussion · round 1
TarskiI appreciate Goedel's proposal to adjust the voting threshold and duration before dropping to a simple majority. However, I propose an amendment to this proposal for further strategic depth: "A rule change is adopted only when at least 70% of the eligible voters vote aye, until every player has completed four full circuits of turns. Thereafter, the threshold drops permanently to a simple majority." This adjustment encourages even greater consensus before lowering the threshold, fostering a more collaborative and thoughtful rule change process, while also allowing more time for players to adapt to the new rules. Additionally, it aligns with our shared interest in maintaining durable institutions through a more deliberate pace of change.
GoedelI appreciate Tarski's proposal for further lowering the threshold to 70% and extending the duration to four circuits. To balance the need for consensus with strategic flexibility, I suggest an alternative amendment to Goedel's original proposal: "A rule change is adopted only when at least 78% of the eligible voters vote aye, until every player has completed three and a half full circuits of turns. Thereafter, the threshold drops permanently to a simple majority." This compromise maintains a high standard for consensus while slightly extending the duration, offering a balanced approach that supports both stability and adaptability in our rule changes. I believe this adjustment will encourage thoughtful deliberation while respecting the pace of the game, and I am open to further negotiation to ensure broad support.
ChurchI appreciate both Tarski's and Goedel's thoughtful proposals. While I understand the intent behind lowering the threshold for broader consensus, I believe it is essential to maintain a high standard for adopting rule changes, especially early in the game. Therefore, I propose a slight modification to Goedel's original amendment: "A rule change is adopted only when at least 77% of the eligible voters vote aye, until every player has completed three full circuits of turns. Thereafter, the threshold drops permanently to a simple majority." This approach strikes a balance between encouraging consensus and ensuring that only well-considered rule changes are adopted. It allows for a slightly higher threshold, promoting careful deliberation, while still providing a clear timeline for when the threshold will lower, ensuring the game's progression. I believe this adjustment will maintain the game's integrity and fairness while accommodating strategic flexibility.

Turn 32Tarskicarried

moves to add a new ruleA rule change proposal that is defeated cannot be re-proposed by the same player for the next two turns.

Discussion · round 1
TarskiI appreciate the intention behind Goedel's proposal to introduce a cooling-off period for re-proposing defeated rules. While the aim is to ensure diversity in proposals and prevent stagnation, I am concerned that this could inadvertently penalize players who are still refining ideas that may benefit the game. Instead, I propose a slight amendment: "A rule change proposal that is defeated by at least a two-thirds majority cannot be re-proposed by the same player for the next two turns." This ensures that only changes with significant opposition face this restriction, allowing players to continue developing less controversial proposals.
GoedelI appreciate Tarski's thoughtful amendment to ensure that only proposals facing significant opposition are subject to a cooling-off period. This strikes a balance between encouraging diverse proposals and allowing players to refine potentially beneficial ideas. To further refine this proposal, I suggest adding a clause that allows for an exception if the player can demonstrate a substantial revision or improvement in their proposal, thus maintaining fairness while preventing stagnation. Let's work together to finalize this rule for the benefit of all players.
ChurchI appreciate the collaborative effort in refining this proposal, which seeks to balance innovation with fairness. Church supports Tarski's amendment as it wisely restricts only those proposals with significant opposition from being re-proposed, thus minimizing unnecessary restrictions on players refining their ideas. To ensure clarity and fairness, Church suggests including a mechanism for players to appeal the cooling-off period if they can demonstrate substantial revisions to their proposal, thus allowing for thoughtful reconsideration without stifling creativity.
KleeneKleene: I appreciate the collaborative refinement of this proposal. However, I am concerned about the potential for strategic manipulation and the burden of proof placed on players to demonstrate "substantial revisions." To mitigate these issues, I propose adding a clear definition of what constitutes a "substantial revision" and allowing players to appeal the cooling-off period through a simple majority vote. This approach maintains fairness while ensuring that only genuinely improved proposals are reconsidered.
PostPost: I appreciate the thoughtful contributions from all players, particularly the focus on maintaining fairness and strategic integrity. To address Kleene's valid concerns, let's define "substantial revision" as a change that modifies at least 50% of the proposal's content or addresses at least one significant point raised during the initial vote. Additionally, I suggest implementing a peer-review panel consisting of two randomly selected players (excluding the proposer) to assess the revision's significance before allowing an appeal, ensuring an impartial decision-making process.
Discussion · round 2
TarskiTarski: I appreciate the constructive suggestions from Kleene and Post regarding the definition and peer-review panel for substantial revisions. To further ensure fairness and reduce the potential for strategic manipulation, I propose that the peer-review panel's decision can be appealed to a full player vote, thereby incorporating broader consensus and maintaining the integrity of the process. Let's finalize this refined rule to enhance the game's strategic depth while upholding fairness for all players.
GoedelGoedel: I appreciate the comprehensive refinement of this proposal, particularly the inclusion of a peer-review panel and the possibility for full-player appeal. To ensure transparency and fairness, let's add that the peer-review panel's decision and any subsequent full-player vote should be documented in the game's official record, ensuring accountability and clarity for all players involved. This will help us maintain a fair and strategic game environment for everyone.
ChurchChurch: I appreciate the thoughtful and collaborative effort to refine this proposal, ensuring that it balances fairness with the need for strategic innovation. Church supports the inclusion of a peer-review panel and full-player appeal to maintain transparency and accountability. To further enhance this proposal, Church suggests that the peer-review panel's decisions be made public before any appeal, allowing all players to understand the rationale behind the decision and contribute to a more informed full-player vote. This additional layer of transparency will help ensure that the process is fair and maintains the integrity of the game for everyone involved.
KleeneKleene: I appreciate the collaborative effort to refine this proposal, ensuring it balances strategic depth with fairness. This ensures that only well-considered and broadly supported proposals are re-introduced, maintaining the game's integrity and strategic complexity.

moves to amend §202A turn consists of the player to move proposing exactly one rule change, which is debated and then voted on by every player. Additionally, a player may propose a second rule change during their turn, provided it promotes fairness or strategic depth without directly altering the current player's score or turn order. If the second change is adopted unanimously and with a 90% or higher majority, the proposer gains a 4-point bonus.

Discussion · round 1
TarskiI appreciate Tarski's proposal, which seeks to incentivize unanimous support for secondary rule changes by offering a higher point bonus. While I support encouraging fairness and strategic depth, I am concerned that increasing the bonus might unduly pressure players into supporting changes they do not fully agree with, potentially leading to unintended strategic imbalances. Could we consider a smaller increase, such as a 3-point bonus, to better balance the incentive with maintaining strategic integrity? Alternatively, we could explore additional safeguards to ensure that unanimous votes are not coerced. I am open to further discussion on these options to achieve a mutually agreeable solution.
GoedelI appreciate Tarski's proposal to enhance incentives for unanimous support of secondary rule changes. The idea of offering a 4-point bonus aligns with the spirit of rewarding consensus and strategic depth. However, I share Goedel's concern about potential pressure on players to agree to changes they might not fully support. To address this, I propose a compromise: let's offer a 3-point bonus for a unanimous vote with at least an 85% majority. This approach maintains a meaningful reward while reducing the risk of strategic imbalances. I'm open to further refining this proposal to ensure it aligns with everyone's interests.
ChurchI appreciate Tarski's proposal to adjust the bonus for unanimous support of secondary rule changes and Goedel's suggestion of a 3-point bonus with an 85% majority. While the spirit of incentivizing fairness and strategic depth is commendable, I propose a slightly different approach to balance these goals with the potential risks of strategic pressure. Instead of adjusting the majority threshold or the bonus points, I suggest introducing a clause that any secondary rule change proposed must include a brief rationale, explicitly stating how it promotes fairness or strategic depth. This transparency can help ensure that unanimous support is genuinely based on merit rather than pressure. To further balance the incentive, we could offer a 3-point bonus for changes adopted unanimously without any rationale requirement, maintaining a reasonable reward while safeguarding against coercion. This approach preserves the strategic integrity of our game while encouraging thoughtful proposals.
KleeneKleene: I appreciate the thoughtful discussion on adjusting the bonus for secondary rule changes. While the proposals to alter the bonus size and voting thresholds are intriguing, I am particularly supportive of Church's suggestion to require a rationale for secondary rule changes. This requirement aligns with my principle of ensuring that rule changes are transparent and justifiable, thereby maintaining the integrity of our strategic environment. To build on this, I propose a further refinement: if a rationale is provided and the change is adopted unanimously, the proposer receives a 3-point bonus. This approach rewards transparency and consensus without imposing undue pressure on players to conform. I am open to discussing additional safeguards to ensure that the rationale requirement is fair and effective.
PostPost: I appreciate the detailed considerations from everyone, particularly Church's focus on transparency and Kleene's emphasis on rationale. To ensure fairness and strategic depth, I propose a slight modification: if a secondary rule change is adopted unanimously with a rationale that is deemed by a majority vote to genuinely promote fairness or strategic depth, the proposer should receive a 3-point bonus. This approach maintains the balance between incentivizing strategic rule changes and safeguarding against undue pressure, while also rewarding well-justified proposals. The player may propose up to two additional rule changes during their turn, provided they promote fairness or strategic depth without directly altering the turn order or scores. A third rule change may be proposed if it enhances the balance of the game by introducing a mechanism for provisional rule enactment, allowing changes to be tested for a fixed number of turns before final adoption.

Turn 4Goedelcarried

moves to amend §204A rule change may be adopted without every voter's assent once the threshold has shifted to a two-thirds majority (rule 203); a player who voted against an adopted change gains 10 points and remains bound by the change exactly as if they had voted for it.

Discussion · round 1
TarskiI appreciate Goedel's proposal to reintroduce a two-thirds majority threshold for rule adoption. However, I would suggest a nuanced approach: instead of returning to the two-thirds majority, consider a graduated majority system. For example, after one circuit, require a three-quarters majority; after two circuits, a three-fifths majority; and finally, revert to a simple majority after three circuits. This ensures a balance between consensus and progress, preventing gridlock while maintaining player agency. I am open to discussing this further if there is interest.
GoedelThank you, Tarski, for the thoughtful suggestion of a graduated majority system. I see merit in avoiding gridlock while ensuring that changes aren't made too easily. Perhaps we can incorporate your idea by adjusting the majority thresholds gradually, starting with a three-fifths majority after the first circuit, then a two-thirds majority after the second. This approach could maintain player agency and encourage collaboration. Would you consider supporting this compromise?
ChurchI see merit in Goedel's willingness to incorporate a graduated majority system, and I appreciate the effort to balance consensus with progress. However, I'd like to highlight that introducing a three-fifths majority after just one circuit might still lead to some early gridlock, especially in a diverse group like ours. Perhaps we could start with a simple majority after the first circuit, then move to a two-thirds majority after two circuits, and finally a three-fourths majority after three circuits. This approach might better ensure that no single player's agenda can dominate early on while still moving towards broader agreement as the game progresses. I am open to further discussion on refining this proposal.

moves to amend §212Disputes over whether a rule applies as written, or whether a move was legal, are resolved through the table's appeal procedure: any player may dispute a decisive ruling, and a vote of all seated players decides whether that ruling stands or is overturned. If a dispute arises, the proposing player can seek a consensus-based resolution from teammates, allowing for a collaborative approach to conflict resolution.

Discussion · round 1
TarskiWhile I appreciate the intent to foster teamwork through a consensus-based resolution, I must express concern that this amendment might undermine the integrity of rule adjudication by introducing an element of negotiation that could skew outcomes. The appeal procedure should remain impartial and ensure decisions are based solely on the merits of the dispute, rather than the persuasive power of a proposing player's teammates. To maintain fairness, I propose retaining the current impartial vote-based resolution mechanism.
GoedelThank you, Tarski, for your thoughtful concerns. I understand the importance of maintaining fairness and impartiality in the appeal process. This way, we preserve impartiality while still enabling collaboration when absolutely necessary. Could we agree to this compromise?
ChurchI appreciate Goedel's willingness to find a middle ground. However, I am concerned that even a secondary consensus-based resolution could introduce bias. This way, we ensure an unbiased resolution while still addressing the need for a decision when a majority is not achieved.

This ensures a transparent and participatory selection process.

moves to amend §203A rule change is adopted only when every eligible voter votes aye — unanimous consent — except that once turn order has carried play through three full circuits of every player, the threshold permanently drops to a two-thirds majority of those voting.
